# A Complex Legacy

THE EUROPEAN PRESENCE IN AFRICAN ART

Olowe's images of the British Captain Ambrose (see pp.767 and 770) may be compared to earlier images of Europeans in the court art of Benin, where the Portuguese had been granted trading concessions in the late fifteenth century. Portuguese soldiers who had served the oba or king of Benin in campaigns against the neighbouring states were shown with their muskets and cross-bows in brass statuettes and plaques incorporated in the decoration of the royal palace. Bearded faces of Portuguese form the crown of an ivory mask probably carved to be worn on the hip of the oba in commemorative rites for his mother, who had witnessed the first arrival of Europeans (12,27). Such images indicated the power of the ruling family.

By the late nineteenth century relations between Africans and Europeans in this part of the continent had undergone a complete change. Although Europeans had secured no more than footholds on the coast from which to conduct the Atlantic slavetrade, they initiated a spiral of destruction in which African rulers became involved in wars with one another to obtain captives to sell to the slave-traders. In exchange they were given fire-arms which they used in further wars. The abolition of the slavetrade by the European powers in the early nineteenth century helped to bring peace to a ravaged country while, at the same time, opening it up to other forms of exploitation. In 1851, for instance, the British signed an anti-slave-trade treaty with the king of Lagos, an island just off the West African coast, which was constituted a British colony a decade later. From there trading companies could penetrate into the interior and in the course of the next half century the whole of modern Nigeria became a British protectorate in which local rulers were nominally independent but ceded some rights in return for protection from neighbouring states, especially the colonies of ether European powers. Captain Ambrose appears to have been the first British colonial official to visit Ikere and other small Yoruba states in the interior. By the time Olowe carved his image on the door (18.34), however, Nigeria had been formally designated a colony in which local rulers retained little of their former power. The doors were sent to London to be shown in the British Empire Exhibition of 1924 and were acquired for the British Museum from the ogoga, who commissioned Olowe to carve another pair.

A nearly life-size wooden statue of a European soldier was carved, for an unknown purpose, in about 1900 by a sculptor of the Baule group of people living in the French Ivory Coast colony (5). His heart-shaped face resembles those on masks carved in the same region. But the sculptor took pains to record details of costume, tropical helmet, tight-fitting uniform, medal and boots. His proportions and stiff military pose, standing to attention, are quite unlike those of the Baule or any other African images that were so avidly collected in early twentieth-century Europe. Whether commissioned by a European or an African, this work reveals the sculptor's flexibility and his ability to adapt his style for a novel task. The same may be said of figures of Europeans carved for export on ivory tusks from the mid-nineteenth century in the region of the Zaire estuary (now the Democratic Republic of Congo). On one, several Europeans, including two men in conversation - probably a merchant and a store-keeper holding a huge key - and another seated in a canoe, have a somewhat comic look but are rendered with the same attention to characteristic costumes and facial expressions as the many Africans surrounding them (18,36; 36b).

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/2f571d2a637c3998a3b3912f340fdb7772eb6a275c3bff5c7db4f93e8fc1d0c5.jpg)  
5 Anonymous Baule sculptor, French Colonial Officer, c.goo. Carved and painted wood. 4ft 10%/ins (1.49m) high. Staatliches Museum fur Völkerkunde, Munich.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/173727d98a68e54de43a674d5948d79b2d840f144e04d4353b35ab343b492b74.jpg)

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/74cd7fdcbbd817eac52c2f9a86d82aa769800e925fa84cafa22da49575431194.jpg)  
Anonymous carver in Cabinda or Loango.Africans and Europeans, C.850-go0Carved vory tusk, total length 27ins (68.5cm). Staatliches Museum für Volkerkunde, Munich.

In the period after World War II, which has seen African states gaining political independence from their former colonial rulers, the colonial era has left a complex, frequently troubled legacy. African artists have adapted both indigenous traditions and Western art styles to engage with contemporary political and social reality. The South African artist Willie Bester (b. 1956), for example, used the technique of assemblage - common to both avantgarde Western art and African art forms - to create Semekazi (Migrant Miseries) (18.53), which records the hardships suffered by the black worker Semekazi under the apartheid system imposed by the white minority government of South Africa before democratic majority rule was finally achieved in 1994.

Digitized by Google

Original from UNIVERSITY OF VIRGINIA

Olowe also represented Ambrose riding a horse on a door carved for another palace (now in the Nigerian Museum, Lagos). In the same period, different styles were adopted by other Yoruba sculptors whose names are recorded. They all specialized in secular works which permitted greater individuality of handling than cult images associated with the spirit world and initiatory and other rituals.

Although both iron and wood are highly perishable in the tropics, and body paintings are ephemeral, considerable evidence is nevertheless available about the history of the arts in which Africans sought to express their relationship with fellow beings, the natural environment, and the forces of good and evil, life and death. Some symbolic motifs clearly remained unchanged over long periods, as in the arts of Europe and Asia. A wooden divination board (opon ifa) acquired by a German collector in the mid-seventeenth century (Ulm Museum) is very similar to others carved and used more than 3oo years later; indeed, traditional Yoruba divination (ifa) is still practised (). But artistic and other cultural traditions were also subject to change. African art of the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries was the product of a long process of development, conditioned by extensive migrations, by the rise and fall of states, by the introduction of materials and techniques from outside, and by the work of individual artists.

Continuity and change are evident in uniquely African power figures of the type that used to be called fetishes (a term now obsolete in this context on account of the entirely different meaning given to it in psychological studies). They were products of collaboration between sculptors, patrons who wished to control natural or supernatural forces and diviners who provided substances to which spiritual or supernatural properties were traditionally ascribed. Such figures often incorporate pieces of mirror glass taken to Africa no earlier than the sixteenth century, usually much later. It was believed that evil spirits of malevolent human beings would be repelled by reflections of themselves, or else that the mirror would, so to speak, absorb the image of the looker who would then be destroyed by the medicines embedded in the power figure. Mirror glass gave a more perfect reflection than pieces of polished stone or shell which had probably been used before it became available.

raditional Yoruba divination in Nigeia  
![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/9a57d964fcd9d646fdff8700bccaadcebe2ff573a81dd4d92a0990de900f53bd.jpg)

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/da7171a8a9579aeb4c310b62b22655f22852b636d743a631da01a6db509cb8f3.jpg)  
The Agbeni Shango shrine Ibadan, Nigeria.Photograph 910.

Like the reliquaries of Buddhist and Christian cults, power figures were revered and often housed in shrines alongside other treasured objects. A shrine to the Oyo thunder god Shango, photographed in 1910, shows a symbolic frieze supported by the carved house-posts of the sort used in royal palaces (,). Inside the shrine can be seen a variety of finely crafted artifacts - leather bags with appliqué decoration, sculpture, pottery and textiles. As in other religions, sacred sculptures were sometimes primarily symbolic, sometimes actually worshipped and believed to be living. They might be discarded if they were found to be ineffective. Some are superb works of art, none more striking than that from Mayombe (Zaire) (). The head of a young man is carved with sensitive naturalism and set among swathes of leather and skins and feathers, royal insignia that suggest the power of the substances embedded behind the mirror-glass eyes. Other power figures, called nkondi in the Kikongo language, bristle with iron nails driven into them to activate their power, to cure or to kill, but also as a kind of solemn oath confirming a contract between merchants, for example, or declaring a truce in warfare . The use of these nails, of European Power figure (nduda), from Mayombe, Zaire, pre83.Wood,glass, high. Staatliches Museum für Volkerkunde, Munich.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/c3aea03935412907941054510c0f35c0ffec9407f6da73b1a71962c72785f1d9.jpg)

make, possibly replaced earlier wooden pegs, and it has been suggested that the idea of investing them with supernatural potency was inspired by crucifixes or reliquaries  in the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries Christianity was the state religion of the kingdom of Kongo (on either side of the Zaire river) where these figures have been found.

Iron had, however, a much older symbolic significance derived from its use in agriculture, hunting and warfare some centuries before the birth of Christ (see p. 127). In traditional African societies smelters and forgers of iron belonged to a distinct hereditary caste and in some there was a close association between metallurgy and kingship, connected also with notions of nature, fertility and political control. This context for metalworking tended to inhibit large-scale production, and increasing quantities of pigiron were imported. It was, indeed, partly to obtain it that Africans became embroiled in the vicious spiral of the Atlantic slave-trade, selling captives for metal that could be forged into weapons to obtain more captives. Among the Yoruba, users of iron implements worship the god Ogun, who introduced the metal to their ancestors, and wear iron amulets (formerly including miniature slave manacles). The neighbouring Fon ethnic group in Dahomey (now the Republic of Benin) worshipped the god of iron and war under the name of Gu. And here, in about 186o, King Glele (1858-89) commissioned a life-size statue representing - though not portraying - himself and his father as a warrior

Digitized by Google in the guise of Gu, with a large sword in each hand and a crown of miniature weapons and tools on his head (). T i , although its angularity and asymmetrical pose are uncharacteristic of the continent generally, as is the technique of piecing iron together. This statue is, in fact, unique: the work of a sculptor-smith said to have been Akate Akpele Kendo who was taken prisoner of war in King Glele' first military victory and then set up in a si' cmpund jusutsde he royal palac  Abomy. With rods and sheets of scrap metal, hammered and

Akate Akpele Kendo. Warrior, c. 186o. Iron,5t 5ins (.65m) high. Museé de I'Homme, Paris.

twisted and bent, he created an image in which the symbolism of the material itself and the form so forcefully given to it have equal importance. The power to bring victory in war was ascribed to it and it was predominantly housed in a military shrine surrounded by large-scale weapons in the palace when not being carried to a battlefield to threaten and menace Glele's enemies. It was hailed there as Agoije and was thought to yell'Watch out' as it advanced.

The creation of wood sculptures was in many parts of Africa accompanied by rituals, from the felling of a tree and propitiation of its spirit to some kind of sacrifice on the work's completion. The predominantly curving, organic forms of African sculpture in wood are ordered and controlled by simplification and balance even in representations of figures in motion, as in the half life-size statues from the Bangwa kingdom in the Cameroon grasslands  the work of a single sculptor, though whether they were intended to complement one another is uncertain . The male figure represents a king with an elaborate head-dress and a necklaceof leopardclaws, holding in one hand a phallic shaped container for palmwine  symbols of royal power. The female figure wears a cowrie necklace and bracelet and carries a rattle; these attributes identify her as a mother of twins and thus a woman who has been accorded an important position in a Cameroon palace and takes a leading role in fertility rituals. Although these works do not come from the Yoruba region, they have aesthetic qualities valued by the Yoruba in sculpture: modified naturalism, 'visibility' or clear articulation of parts, and shining surfaces which afford a play of fickering light and shade. The woman especially has the flexibility of body and the coolness or detachment of expression particularly admired in dance.

Similar qualities are apparent in the statuette of a woman with a baby in her lap from an entirely different culture, that of the Yombe living in Zaire (,). This type of mother-and-child sculpture, termed pfemba, may reveal the

A king and a mother of twins, from the kingdom of Bangwa, Cameroon, late 19th or early 2oth century. Wood, 34/ and 34%4ins (87.6 and 87cm) high. Private collection.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/f409342d453c16fa11a6e252907cc8f7b8eb826df02e28391dedcd746c397456.jpg)

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/c00dcf8392a5c56bb0bd631d0bf6d8821786a885999b440b3a4ff26701c3ea1b.jpg)  
, Maternity Figure, from Zaire, pre-1913. Yombe. Wood, 10ins (25cm) high. Rietberg Museum, Zürich.

influence of Christian images of the Virgin Mary with the Christ Child. It presents a personification of idealized the shoulders. That the social structure of Yombe clans was matrilineal  descent being traced through the maternal not paternal line  is no coincidence. The statuette is one of several that seem to be the work of a single sculptor. But some maternity figures from the same region are remarkably diverse in treatment, and the different groups have been ascribed to unnamed sculptors whose personal styles are s istinctive s those of the Europeanists who depicted or carved images of Mary and the Christ Child femba figures provide a clear indication of the role played by individual artists in Africa - as much as anywhere else in the world.

Reliquary guardian figures (mbulu-ngulu) created by the Kota in Gabon could hardly be more different in their flatness, spare geometrical symbolism and apparent negation of individuality as well as naturalism (,). They were carved in wood covered with sheets and bands of copper to be placed over wickerwork baskets (bwete) containing the skulls and bones of chiefs, notable warriors, founder ovillage an ohe tiguishe ntors. Not intended to represent the people whose relics they guarded, they are striking images of quite literally disembodied spirits whose eyes appear to stare fixedly

from their elliptical faces. These are crowned by a crest in the form of the crescent moon and have wing-like elements on either side; angled struts below the head, which may be interpreted as arms, served to attach the figure to its basket. Within this basic form there is much variation in the treatment of the surface and the shape - engraved or modelled in very low relief  the use of copper wire and sometimes sheets of brass as well as copper providing a contrast in colors.

Whereas in Europe, literacy has tended to destroy the instinctive orchestration of the senses and to focus attention on the static image as on a statement in black and white, in Africa, as in north-west America and Melanesia (though curiously not Polynesia), much artistic activity was devoted to masks made to be seen in movement as part of a ceremonial often accompanied by music. Performance, which combines diverse arts in a dynamic enactment, is seen by many as the African art form par excellence. Masks were worn in rites with various purposes, promote human fertility and to increase food supply in a world where uncertainty was always a menacing presence, and to ensure the continuity and stability of a social group, initiating the young into its traditions and preserving its myths by dramatic presentation. Some masks were worn on the top of the head so that they could be seen only by

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/0f827c892d572aa31b4e023fe8a360a498a1c298fbb057234f02bd4a0d4aa419.jpg)  
8, Kota reliquary figure. Wood and copper sheeting. 263ins (68cm) high. British Museum, London.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/d954a548b977a3ebe43a1acabccc88faf5cb001f17f92439d39a4f4c2a20c0b6.jpg)  
The goddess Nimba, from Guinea, late 1gth century. Carved wood with coppernails and natural fibres ins .m)h Musée de I'Homme, Paris.

the spirits of the air, but generally they were visible to all participants and set off by elaborate costumes concealing the wearers who were completely transformed into animated spirits. Among the Baga of Guinea, heavy carved wood busts of Nimba, goddess of maternity, were at the time of the rice harvest carried on the shoulders of dancers who could see out through holes in the breasts but who were hidden by rustling skirts of fibres ().

A mask from the Sassandra river area of the Ivory Coast was made for the Poro society, a male organization that drec diver publicncins, nitatory,civic,elus, administrative, judicial and social (,). It is roughly composed of unnaturalistic and non-representational forms which are given the appearance of a terrifying human head by their arrangement  a flat oblong panel with a semi-circular block for a brow, cylinders for eyes, a vertical strip for a nose. A photograph taken in 1972 or 1975 shows four Dogon dancers transformed into powerful

spirits called Sirige, by masks with immensely tall superstructures, followed by two other dancers with masks of a female spirit celebrating the courage of women (). Women fought alongside men. Such masks had the power to capture and control life-forces when activated in ritual dances in which the skill of their makers and the adroitness of their wearers were of equal importance. Many are also powerful works of sculpture: notably the sombrely

, Ceremonial mask, from Wobé or Grebo, Ivory Coast, late th century. Painted wood, feathers and fibres,11ins (28cm) high. Musée de I'Homme, Paris.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/f36cc26a49d352d91c3e228d54a1a2b1fa11e55afbda7f37a11fb81060698269.jpg)

impresive Black Monkey mask carved, like the Sge masks, to be worn in dances which terminated a period of mourning (18,48).

Some masks were preserved in treasuries and brought out for use in such important ceremonies as initiation rites and royal installations. Elaborate examples embellished with copper, cowrie shells and beads were made in the small but rich kingdom of Kuba, in the Kanai river region of the central Congo (Zaire), a stable state with an oral tradition of a succession of rulers dating back to the seventeenth century (corroborated by references to Halley's comet). One of a set of three masks, worn to enact a myth of the origin of royal power, personifies Mbwoom, a Pygmy, who fights with his elder brother, the king, for the affections of their sister - though Mbwoom is identified also with commoners in the realm and the sister with

women in general (). This is, in fact, a specimen of court art, marked by refinement of craftsmanship as well as costliness of embellishment. But in a few uncentralized small-scale social groups where masks were assembled for specific occasions others were carved in wood to be handed down from one generation to another. Those carved by the Dan on the Ivory Coast (Côte d'Ivoire) to embody an ideal of feminine beauty correspond with aesthetic preferences verbally expressed by the sculptors as symmetry about a vertical axis with balance, rhythm and harmony between their various masses, surfaces and lines (,50).

Even when carved masks are exhibited in isolation in museum displays, their strong three-dimensional qualities, which have appealed so much to European and American artists since the early twentieth century, survive. It remains true, however, that the arts of Africa, like those of Oceania, Australia and the American North-West Coast, lose their all-important fourth dimension when uprooted and taken out of their ritual contexts - just as people living in urbanized and industrialized societies tend to lose their sixth sense. In recent decades, although many traditional art forms are no longer in viable ceremonial use, African cultural heritage is central to the work of contemporary African artists working in Africa and abroad. The arts of masquerade have been reinterpreted, for example, by the Kalabari sculptor Sokari Douglas Camp (b. 1958). She used

Traditions have been maintained in the construction and adornment of mbari (see p.765) although the rendering of their paintings and sculptures has been modernized, just as

Digitized by Google corrugated iron has replaced thatch for roofing (). Techniques that originated in Europe and the USA but which became available world-wide, have been adopted by many African artists including, for instance, Willie Bester whose mixed media Semekazi of 1993 (,53), recording the plight of a worker under apartheid (see p. 763), exemplifies the continuing relationship between artistic production, social and political circumstances.
